<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Projections of population growth</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:299px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/297px-World_population_v3.svg.png" width="297" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/446px-World_population_v3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/595px-World_population_v3.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="537" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World population estimates from 1800 to 2100, based on "high", "medium" and "low" <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://esa.un.org/wpp/unpp/panel_population.htm">United Nations projections in 2015</a>  and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://esa.un.org/unpd/wpp/">UN historical estimates</a> for pre-1950 data.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Projections of population growth</b> established in 2017 predict that the human population is likely to keep growing until 2100,<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> reaching an estimated 8.6 billion in 2030, 9.8 billion in 2050 and 11.2 billion by 2100, while the 7 billion milestone was reached in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-WM-Forecast_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WM-Forecast-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a> follows its course worldwide, the population will age significantly, with most countries outside Africa trending towards a rectangular <a href="/wiki/Age_pyramid" class="mw-redirect" title="Age pyramid">age pyramid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Census-Aging-2015_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census-Aging-2015-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> is currently growing by approximately 83 million people each year.<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Growth rates are slowing to various extents within different populations with result of the overall <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> rate decreasing from 1.55% per year in 1995 to 1.25% in 2005, 1.18% in 2015 and 1.10% in 2017. The median estimate for future growth sees the world population reaching 8.6 billion in 2030, 9.8 billion in 2050 and 11.2 billion by 2100<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> assuming a continuing decrease in average <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> from 2.5 births per woman in 2010–2015 to 2.2 in 2045–2050 and to 2.0 in 2095–2100, according to the medium-variant projection.<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a> trending towards uniform and stable values worldwide, the main driver of future population growth is the evolution of the fertility rate.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup>
</p><p>While most scenarios still predict continued growth into the 22nd century, there is a roughly 27% chance that the total population could stabilize or begin to fall before 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> Longer-term speculative scenarios over the next two centuries can predict anything between runaway growth to radical decline (36.4 billion or 2.3 billion people in 2300), with the median projection showing a slight decrease followed by a stabilization around 9 billion people.<sup id="cite_ref-un-esa1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un-esa1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>13</span></sup>
</p><p>By 2070, the bulk of the world's population growth is predicted to take place in Africa: of the additional 2.4 billion people projected between 2015 and 2050, 1.3 billion will be added in Africa, 0.9 billion in Asia and only 0.2 billion in the rest of the world. Africa's share of global population is projected to grow from 16% in 2015 to 25% in 2050 and 39% by 2100, while the share of Asia will fall from 60% in 2015 to 54% in 2050 and 44% in 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> The strong growth of the African population will happen regardless of the rate of decrease of fertility, because of the exceptional proportion of young people already living today. For example, the UN projects that the population of <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> will surpass that of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> The population of the more developed regions is slated to remain mostly unchanged, at 1.2 billion, as international migrations from high-growth regions compensate the fertility deficit of richer countries.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>11</span></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_regions">Growth regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Growth regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/300px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" width="300" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/450px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/600px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries by fertility rate (2018), according to <a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></div></div></div>
<p>From 2017 to 2050, nine countries are (<i>in most country population is very low but based on growth</i>) expected to account for half of the world's projected population increase: <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, listed according to the expected size of their contribution to that projected population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2010 to 2015, the average world fertility was 2.5 children per woman,<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> about half the level in 1950–1955 (5 children per woman). In the medium variant, global fertility is projected to decline further to 2.2 in 2045 to 2050 and to 2.0 in 2095-2100.<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Global life expectancy at birth, which is estimated to have risen from 46 years in 1950–1955 to 65 years in 2000–2005, is expected to keep rising to reach 75 years in 2045–2050. In the more developed regions, the projected increase is from 75 years today to 82 years by mid-century. Among the least developed countries, where life expectancy today is just under 50 years, it is expected to be 66 years in 2045–2050. The population of 31 countries or areas, including Ukraine, Romania, Japan and most of the <a href="/wiki/Former_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Former Soviet Union">successor states of the Soviet Union</a>, is expected to be lower in 2050 than in 2005.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projected_migration_to_developed_countries">Projected migration to developed countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Projected migration to developed countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the United Nations, during 2005–2050 the net number of international migrants to more developed regions is projected to be 98 million. Because deaths are projected to exceed births in the more developed regions by 73 million during 2005–2050, population growth in those regions will largely be due to international migration. In 2000–2005, net migration in 28 countries either prevented <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a> or doubled at least the contribution of natural increase (births minus deaths) to population growth. These countries include Austria, Canada, Croatia, Denmark, Germany, Italy, Portugal, Qatar, Singapore, Spain, Sweden, the United Arab Emirates and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-un.org-pop918_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un.org-pop918-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2050 (medium variant), <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> will have 1.73 billion people, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> 1.46 billion, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> 411 million, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> 398 million, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> 327 million, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> 309 million, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> 265.8 million, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> 232 million, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> 195.3 million, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> 188.5 million, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> 164 million, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> 157.1 million, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> 142 million, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> 133 million, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> 129.4 million, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> 112.8 million, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> 107 million, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> 101 million, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> 96 million, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> 95.5 million, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> 93.5 million, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> 81 million, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> 78 million and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and France 75 million.
<sup id="cite_ref-world2050_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world2050-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_population_in_2050">World population in 2050</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World population in 2050">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_(UN).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/400px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png" width="400" height="355" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/600px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/800px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="1020" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_(UN).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimates of population levels in different <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> between 1950 and 2050, according to the United Nations (2011 edition).  The vertical axis is <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a> and is in millions of people.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pop_continents_2000_2050.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Pop_continents_2000_2050.png/400px-Pop_continents_2000_2050.png" width="400" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Pop_continents_2000_2050.png/600px-Pop_continents_2000_2050.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Pop_continents_2000_2050.png/800px-Pop_continents_2000_2050.png 2x" data-file-width="825" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pop_continents_2000_2050.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>UN estimates (as of 2017) for world population by continent in 2000 and in 2050 (pie chart size to scale).<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><br /> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:yellow; color:yellow;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Asia</span> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:black; color:black;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Africa</span> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:blue; color:blue;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Europe</span> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:red; color:red;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Latin America</span> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:brown; color:brown;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Northern America</span> <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:green; color:green;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Oceania</span></div></div></div>

<p><a href="/wiki/Walter_Greiling" title="Walter Greiling">Walter Greiling</a> projected in the 1950s that world population would reach a peak of about nine billion, in the 21st century, and then stop growing after a readjustment of the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> and a sanitation of the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Estimates published in the 2000s tended to predict that the population of Earth will stop increasing around 2070;<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
In a 2004 long-term prospective report, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Division" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Population Division">United Nations Population Division</a> projected the world population to peak at 9.22 billion in 2075.  After reaching this maximum, it would decline slightly and then resume a slow increase, reaching a level of 8.97 billion by 2300, about the same as the projected 2050 figure.<sup id="cite_ref-un-esa1_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un-esa1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup> 
</p><p>This prediction was revised in the 2010s, to the effect that no maximum will likely be reached in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> 
The main reason for the revision was that the ongoing rapid population growth <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Africa" title="Demographics of Africa">in Africa</a> had been underestimated.
A 2014 paper by demographers from several universities and the United Nations Population Division forecast that the world's population will reach about 10.9 billion in 2100 and continue growing thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-Science_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
The UN as of 2017 predicts a decline of global population growth rate from +1.0% in 2020 to +0.5% in 2050 and to +0.1% in 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgen_Randers" title="Jørgen Randers">Jørgen Randers</a>, one of the authors of the seminal 1972 long-term simulations in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Limits_to_Growth" title="The Limits to Growth">The Limits to Growth</a></i>, offered an alternative scenario in a 2012 book, arguing that traditional projections insufficiently take into account the downward impact of global urbanization on fertility. Randers' "most likely scenario" predicts a peak in the world population in the early 2040s at about 8.1 billion people, followed by decline.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These projected growth patterns depend on assumptions about vital rates. Total fertility is assumed to continue to decline, at varying paces depending on circumstances in individual countries, to a below-replacement level of 1.85 children per woman by mid century. Countries already at this level or below, and other countries when they reach it, will eventually return to replacement over a period of a century and stay at replacement going forward.  All countries are projected to have reached replacement fertility by 2175.
</p><p>Some of the authors of the 2004 UN report say that life expectancy is assumed to rise slowly and continuously. The projections in the report assume this with no upper limit, though at a slowing pace depending on circumstances in individual countries. By 2100, the report assumed life expectancy to be from 66 to 97 years, and by 2300 from 87 to 106 years, depending on the country. Based on that assumption, they said that rising life expectancy will produce small but continuing population growth by the end of the projections, ranging from 0.03 to 0.07 percent annually. The hypothetical feasibility (and wide availability) of <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">life extension</a> by technological means would further exacerbate the overpopulation problem.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Most_populous_nations_by_2030">Most populous nations by 2030</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Most populous nations by 2030">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a more comprehensive list, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_future_population_(United_Nations,_medium_fertility_variant)" title="List of countries by future population (United Nations, medium fertility variant)">List of countries by future population (United Nations, medium fertility variant)</a>.</div>
<p>The UN Population Division has calculated the future population of the world's countries, based on current demographic trends.  The 2015 study projects the world population in 2030 to be 8.5 billion people, with the following estimates for the top 10 countries:<sup id="cite_ref-UN-WPP-2015_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-WPP-2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup>

</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_2050">After 2050</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: After 2050">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Projections for after 2050 have usually assumed that fertility rates will have declined by then and the population will be stable or will decrease. However, a study in 2014 found that fertility rates in Africa have leveled off at around 4.6 instead of continuing to decline, and that consequently world population may be as high as 12 billion by 2100. Reasons for the continuing high birth rate include better <a href="/wiki/Survival_rate" title="Survival rate">survival</a> rates with respect to HIV, and contraception shortage.<sup id="cite_ref-NS_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NS-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Another study, on the other hand, concludes that education of women will lead to low fertility rates even in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-NS_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NS-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">Evolutionary biology</a> also suggests the <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a> may reverse itself; in addition, recent evidence suggests birth rates may be rising in the 21st century in the developed world.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UN projections published in 2017 estimate the world population in 2100 to be 11.2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-esa.un.org_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa.un.org-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_projections_of_the_101_largest_cities_in_the_21st_century">Population projections of the 101 largest cities in the 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Projections_of_population_growth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Population projections of the 101 largest cities in the 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Large <a href="/wiki/Urban_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban areas">urban areas</a> are hubs of economic development and innovation, with larger cities underpinning <a href="/wiki/Regional_economics" title="Regional economics">regional economies</a> and local and global <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> initiatives. Currently, 757 million humans live in the 101 largest cities;<sup id="cite_ref-media.wix.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media.wix.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> these cities are home to 11% of the world’s population.<sup id="cite_ref-media.wix.com_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media.wix.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the century, the world population is projected to grow, with estimates ranging from 6.9 billion to 13.1 billion;<sup id="cite_ref-media.wix.com_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media.wix.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> the percentage of people living in the 101 larger cities is estimated to be 15% to 23%.<sup id="cite_ref-media.wix.com_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media.wix.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following 101 cities with the largest population projections for the years 2025, 2050, 2075, and 2100 are listed below.<sup id="cite_ref-media.wix.com_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media.wix.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
